Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen. This N–H group is a strong hydrogen bond donor, which is crucial for interacting with anionic guests. In many supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their N–H groups can converge on a single anion, forming multiple directional hydrogen bonds. This cooperative binding greatly enhances the affinity and selectivity for anions. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can stabilize the host structure and sometimes modulate the acidity of the N–H protons, further tuning anion binding strength. The design of such hosts often takes inspiration from natural anion-binding motifs, such as those found in biological receptors. Overall, the combination of strong, directional hydrogen bonding and the ability to preorganize multiple pyrrole units makes these hosts highly effective for anion recognition and binding in Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen. This N–H group is a strong hydrogen bond donor, which is crucial for interacting with anionic guests. In many supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their N–H groups can converge on a single anion, forming multiple directional hydrogen bonds. This cooperative binding greatly enhances the affinity and selectivity for anions. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can stabilize the host structure and sometimes modulate the acidity of the N–H protons, further tuning anion binding strength. The design of such hosts often takes inspiration from natural anion-binding motifs, such as those found in biological receptors. Overall, the combination of strong, directional hydrogen bonding and the ability to preorganize multiple pyrrole units makes these hosts highly effective for anion recognition and binding in Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen. This N–H group is a strong hydrogen bond donor, which is crucial for interacting with anionic guests. In many supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their N–H groups can converge on a single anion, forming multiple directional hydrogen bonds. This cooperative binding greatly enhances the affinity and selectivity for anions. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can stabilize the host structure and sometimes modulate the acidity of the N–H protons, further tuning anion binding strength. The design of such hosts often takes inspiration from natural anion-binding motifs, such as those found in biological receptors. Overall, the combination of strong, directional hydrogen bonding and the ability to preorganize multiple pyrrole units makes these hosts highly effective for anion recognition and binding in Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen. This N–H group is a strong hydrogen bond donor, which is crucial for interacting with anionic guests. In many supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their N–H groups can converge on a single anion, forming multiple directional hydrogen bonds. This cooperative binding greatly enhances the affinity and selectivity for anions. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole allows for electronic delocalization, which can stabilize the host structure and sometimes modulate the acidity of the N–H protons, further tuning anion binding strength. The design of such hosts often takes inspiration from natural anion-binding motifs, such as those found in biological receptors.
